Five thousand dollars have been offered
for the detection of the perpetrators of
the robbery by the Selby Smelting Works.
Greater rewards than that will be forth
coming, and when the Pinkertons, the
Morses, the State and county police and
private detectives fall in line for a com
mon purpose the race will be a keen one.
The head employes at the smelting works
hope for and anticipafe the discovery of
the criminals in the near future. No
Treasure Island romance with its charts
mid burled gold gives them a thousht.
Whether as a subterfuge or with afore
thought design red pepper was strewn
frorc the tunnel Into the vault to the
place where It Is supposed the robbers
racde their escape. All traces of pepper
end abruptly on the rocks of the shore
not further than a stone's throw from
the entrance to the vault. Some of the
keen scented detectives sniff a ruse In
this red pepper trail; others declare that
Its purpose was natural â to choke all ca
nine ' "following. And the questions now
propounded to the detective mind are
ones of direction, of escape. Was it by
â¢water or. was It by land? Has the booty
been sunk, or has It been burled, or is It
In possession of the daring criminals?
An<*. as to these- crlmlnals^-did they take
the water route or the land route? ' More
than one mind is puzzling over these prob
lems of detection. The shores of San
Pablo are being scoured by screening
eyes, the hills of Contra Costa and So-
Iheo are being searched and sounded and
the waters watched for strange and sus
picious craft.

The Selby smelting, plant Is a big In
dustry. Thousands and thousands of dol
lars in bullion form are turned out month
ly and after the process from rock to bar
Is completed the heavy, compact - bricks
are stored in vaults. There are two of
these safes in the long, low sheds at
Crockett, one at the north end, the other
at'the south. It. was the former that was
undermined â¢ and entered. Ten ' feet long,
five feet wide and six feet high, this bafe
contained on Monday evening about one
half million dollars in gold and silver.
Month after month the heavy .metal
bricks were rolled Into this vault pre
paratory to a consignment to the Mint.'
Robbery was . never 'thought of,/ for^the
safe was In the big room where the lead
emelters work at the' furnaces â¢ and day
and night shift crews operate. incessantly.
Three watchmen keep a steady vigil. ",The
men who concocted the $280,000 steal" were
well aware that the safe was inaccessible
from the interior â¢ of â¢ the building.-: "With
the noise of passing trains on* the outside
and the rumble and hissing and sizzling
of the furnaces within they, knew. tha,t no
light sound would force attention to their
midnight deed. - So the tunnel was/dug
close to the building, not deeper than
three feet and penetrating no more thau
four feet beyond the outer wall- line.
Then began the task of drilling upward.
First a two-inch plank was penetrated
and sawed away, leaving the thin steel
floor exposed to boring augers. In this
narrow tunnel but one man could work
at a time and he must of necessity have

Some twenty-five or thirty years ago a
robbery on like lines was planned In con
nection with the Sub-Treasury on Com
mercial street, in' this city, but It. was not
carried through. Crooks from the East
came: to San Francisco and rented a place
a door or two above the Sub-Treasury
building which they fitted up as a saloon
at j a cost of several thousand dollars.
"When Â¦well settled and fully acquainted
with the surroundings they started a tun
nel in the cellar of their building, toward
the vaults of the Treasury in which at the
time were nearly 5100,000,000 in gold. Run
ning short of ready cash, they did some
work on the side and thereby aroused sus
picion on' the part of the police, perceiving
which they levanted. On examination of
the place after their departure their work
so far as done was discovered and thfl'ul-'â^
timate design made evident.
Several robberies of banks In the East
by which large amounts were â secured
have been effected by tunneling, but thÂ©
plunder was in the shape of currency and
bends easily carried on the person or in a
hand satchel, but never before has such
a weight cf precious metal been secured
and carried off by cracksmen. The rich
ness of the haul would have made Captain .
Kidd or Sir Walter Raleigh turn green
with envy, even though they worker!,
openly and U3ed cannon -to enforce their
demands. .
"When the session of detectives ended
there was much talk about the robberies I
of other days. In the detective mind the
following noteworthy events in robbery
were lun over again:
At the meeting of detectives last even-
Ing a machinist testified from an expert
viewpoint- that the hole in the bottom of
the safe was cut out by a professional
on account of the mathematical accuracy
of the borings. The plate wa3 lifted off

â¢was bored.
Then the red pepper came up for dissua

into . the eyes of any possible disturber
during the busy hours of the night when
the gold was removed. An inquisition of
the storekeepers in Vallejo Junction ami
Crockett revealed nothing in regard to
the purchase of an extraordinary quantity
of red pepper.
Two theories of escape were discussed.
The detectives have divided themselves
into the land sharps and the water sharps.
The upholders of a water escape baae
their judgment on the 'trail of pepper to
the water's edge, the tying of the boat
to the piles nearby and the relict of goM
bricks. The other partisans bristle with
the belief that the gold and the men
whose backs must ache from the packing
of It have turned Inland. Sunken treas
ure or escape up the river or out into the
deep waters have no. charm for them.
Their "ideas run to burialâ an earth con
cealmentâand a hiding. Thinking that
the gang who so boldly and successfully
accomplished the robbery must nave

struck a rigid search will be made for a

.The young woman, was tying her horsa
near the corner of State and Orange
streets when the animal became fright
ened at some plumes on her hat and be
gan to back. The young woman was an
gry and struck the horse with the strap
she was tying him with. She had no more
than done so when he Jumped at her,
rearing in the air.
Miss Swartz was so badly frightened
she was unable to move as the animal
sprang upon her. The brute's Jaws closed
with a snap on her face, - and her Jaw,
lips, nose and brows were fearfully lacer
ated. There were many men almost
within an arm's length of the girl, but
none realized her peril in time to save
her from .the brute's furv, although he
was thrown to the ground and held pros
trate by twenty captors before the faint
ing woman fell to the pavement. Surgical
assistance was at once called and every
effort was made to compose Miss
Swartz's features in a way to prevent the
most serious disfigurement. The attend
ing physicians fear lockjaw, but say that
If this does not set in the young woman
will recover from the shock of her dread
ful experience, though she must always
bear the marks of the horse's teeth on
her face. '

_ Special Dispatch to The Call.
To-day the Pacific Coast Company,
through Its attorney. L. L. Cory, com
menced suit against the J. B. Inderrleden
Company; to recover possession of 660.CCT
pounds of raisins valued at $34,947 57, and
damages to the amount of $5000. If delivery
of the raisins cannot be had plaintiff aska
judgment for the value of the goods.
About two weeks â¢ ago the California
Raisin Growers' Association sold its
carry-over of last season's raisms to tha
California Raisin Packing Company. This
company made the purchase with an
agreement that the Pacific Coast Com
pany would take- the contract off its
hands. The deal having been consum
mated the Pacific Company demanded tho
raising, but the holders refused to de
liver them, claiming that the sale of the
raisins had not been legal and that the
growers outside of the "big five" com
bine, or -the Pacific Company, were en
titled to the raisins.
Accordingly yesterday morning about 4
o'clock Lee Gray, Frank Gray. Thomas
Lynch, Henry Pratt and Samuel Samuel
son, representing the "big five," left
Fresno with a force of workmen on a
special train of seven cars and went to
Selma. There they forcibly entered the
Hoyt house and commenced to load the"
raisins on the cars. A watchman was in
charge of the building, but he was asleep
and did not wake up until two cars wera
loaded. He immediately swore to com
plaints charging the men with burglary
and all were arrested. They were released
on bonds of $1000. The work of loading
continued and the train returned to
Fresno about noon with all the raisins.
Considerable litigation Is expected to re
sult from the affair.

BAKERSFIELD, Aug. 6.â A decapitat
ed woman was found last night lying on
the Santa Fe tracks near the junction
with the Southern Pacific about two miles
east of this city by a switching crew of
the Santa Fe. The body was to-day
identified as that of Maude Raymond,
who was employed in a lodging-house in
this city. She was intoxicated last night.
The body was lying between" the tracks

I.ONDON, Aug. 7.â The House of Com
mons last night discussed a vote of, Â£6,
500,000 as a grant in aid of the Transvaal
and Orange River colonies. Sir William
Vernon Harcourt and others severely crit
icized the Government's measures. Cham
berlain, replying, said he quite agreed

the royalist refugees, and he declared that

pose. '

the" vote is for extra r rolling stock. - A

ricultural settlements.
"There is no intention of confiscation.
We have had offers of land at reasonable
prices, and a portion of the vote will be
devoted to the purchase of such lands
with a view of enabling men who volun
teered for the war to settle when the war
is over." V. '.

of the "cruel and arbitrary native labor

ever, Incidents connected with the sailing
of the vessel have caused a suspicion to
arise that the schooner was not anchored
in the bay near the Sausalito shore be
cause of the strike, but because she was
to be used by .the thieves to carry off the
gold bricks. *-
The schooner attracted the attention of
John Hayes, night watchman of the pool
room3 at Sausalito, and of Paul Truett,
schooner was anchored off
Sausalito for several days and but little
attention was paid to her, as it was

place a non-union crew, aboard.
ton iyuj '^ HE mysterious departure of a
'. k from her anchorage In
Ej the bay off Sausallto may fur-

R abouts of the men who' robbed
**â¢ the Selby bullion vault./ The
night watchman of the town, both of

Night Watchman Hayes in discussing
the departure of the vessel last night said:
"Both Truett and myself watched the
schooner for several nights, as we were
anxious to know what success the owners
would meet with in getting a crew for
their vessel. We knew of nothing else but
the lack of a. crew owing to the strike
that wculd" cause them to keep her an
chored where she "was. Since we heard of
the robbery of Selby's vaults, however, we
have thought of several incidents which
cause us to think that she was connected
.with the bullion steal.
"Monday night two men, claiming to be
the captain and mate of the schooner,
came to Sausalito on the 12:15 boat. They
had a small boat at the wharf and jumped
into it and pulled out to the vessel. We
thought they had been across the bay
searching for non-union men and of course
did not pay much attention to them. . .
"About 3:30 a. m. we saw a naphtha
launch coming down the bay from the
direction of Alcatraz Island, heading for
the schooner and- Immediately, came to
the conclusion that the scab crew was on
board. The launch tied>up to the schooner
and remained for some time. The next
sign of life we -Observed was the launch
pulling ahead of the schooner, evidently
towing her.
"Both craft went right out to sea and
we thought no more of the incident until
we heard of the great crime." -
Inquiry along the water front brought
to light the fact that there was but one
schooner anchored off the Sausallto shore
waiting to secure a non-union crew. This
was the schooner Occidental, and she left
the harbor yesterday morning. Arrange
ments had been made with the Red Stack
Company to tow her out this morning,
and a tug was cent over for that purpose.
The Occidental, however, was not at her
anchorage, having taken advantage of-'the
tides and sailed out of the harbor on her
voyage to Humboldt.

drawn No. 2 from tire wheel, had counted
on selecting one of these, but when Wood
made .the selection noted she had to-con
tent herself with a tract south" of "Wood's
and two sections away from the townsite.
Wood's claim is valued at about $50,000.

Several Sub-leaders Deported.
Various rumors as to their disposition
have been circulated In this city to-day,
it being- most generally reported that they
were taken into the Gulf in an unknown
and mysterious vessel. All of the de
ported strike leaders were Spaniards and
Cubans.
The kidnaping last night .had been
planned for days. A great open air meet
ing had been arranged at which- all the
Resistencia leaders .had been Invited, the
purpose being to catch them all in a
bunch. They were all present. Music
had been provided and the streets
were lined with thousands cf peo
ple. Shortly before the posse of citizens
reached the place the news reached the
etrike leaders and they quickly dis
appeared. The crowd was not daunted
and commenced a man-to-man search
through the streets and snatched the men
from the crowds. They were placed in
carriages that had been provided, between
heavily armed guards, and driven, away.
The captured leaders were at once driven
to the County Jail, where they remained
upon the outside -under guard for a few
minutes. Then they disappeared and have
r.ot been seen or heard of since. . j

Crooked Gold Brings Suspicion.
"People who bring gold here to be
coined do not have to tell you where they
got it?" was asked of the superintendent.
"No, they don't have to, but if they re
fused to answer the auestions we ask
they would naturally arouse - suspicion.
We keep a book of records that shows
where all gold conies from- This is kept
for statistics among other purposes.
Further than this, we know about all the
sources of the gold supply and where the
principal mines are. Consequently if a
man brought in a large supply of gold,
especially if it was refined, we would be
curious at once, and if he couldn't tell a
straight story as to where he got it we
would be suspicious. If he refused to an
swer questions at all we would be doubly
suspicious.
any man brought 'crooked'
gold to s. mint something would cause eus
picion to be aroused before he went out.
In ninety-nine cases out of a hundred
where men steal any great amount of gold
they are caught." j
This is what Superintendent Frank
Leach of the United States Branch Mint
in this city said yesterday when question
ed as to the chance the men who robbed
the Selby works had of disposing of any
of their plunder at Uncle Sam's coining
establishments.
WE can't force a man who
comes here to sell bullion to
go to the Government to teir
where it came from, but if

bullion could remelt the bricks that are
stamped if they could find some place
where they could keep a fire going for
their apparatus without being discov
ered."
"Supposing the thieves took the gold
from . the Selby Works to some Mint in
another part of the country â to the one at
Denver or that at Philadelphia. Couldn't
they easily dispose of it?"
"No easier than they could here. They'd
arouse suspicion. No, the /act that the
gold was refined wouldn't necessarily
show that it was stolen from a smelter,
though the smelters pay the owners of
ore in coin and not in bullion, for some
mines melt the gold. These are better
known than any others, too. â¢
"Yes, but all sources of gold supply are
so well known that when chunks are
being handled illegitimately suspicion Is
aroused." ' * " ""
"Couldn't bricks be cut up Into small
chunks and sold to pawnbrokers and
others?"
"Well," said the Mint superintendent,
"we'd hang that money up on some pre
text or another until we found out
whether or not we had received 'crooked'
gold."
"But if the man who left the bullion
couldn't be forced to answer questions as
to where he got it and could walk away
with his money at the end of twenty-four
hours, there wouldn't be time in which to
secure evidence upon, which he could be
held, would there?"

SANTA BARBARA, Aug. 6.-Ranger
Carl Stoddard of the Santa Ynez reserva
tion and Joseph Hildreth, a well-known
mountaineer, made a report to-night of
one of the most terrific natural disturb
ances ever known in Californiaâ a great
cloudburst at the head of Mono Creek, in
the north of this county. Although it oc
curred in August it was greater than any
thing ever known in winter months.
For a week it had been damp in the
mountain, and on Sunday there was a
light sprinkling of rain*, Stoddard and
Hildreth were where they could see Mono
Canyon, a gorge fifteen miles long, but
very narrow. Suddenly an intense roar
like thunder was heard from the head of
the mountain. At the head of the can
yon a white line of foam came rushing
down to -the valley. It was a solid and
irresistible wall of water of eieht to
twelve feet high. The roar of the flood
was terrific. It rushed down between
rocky walls over a dry bed. In its wake,
grinding, tumbling, smashing, came thou
sands of bowlders. The rush of waters
lasted for twenty-four hours, delaying
the men from returning to their cabins,
because no one could cross the creek.

JEROME, Ariz.," Aug. 6.â The situation
of the strike is the same as on Saturday
when the men walked out. The smelter is
closed down, with some men at. cleaning
work and repairing. ' A report is abroad
that the miners arc out in this strike, but
this Is not so. There Is no organization
behind the strike. So far as can be learned
the strikers have.no friends other than
what they 'may have individually. W. A.
Clark left yesterday for New York and
before going announced that his orders
were that the management should stand
pat and make no concessions. The gen
eral feeling in Jerome is that the trouble
will be settled within seven days.

The medal is in recognition of heroic
conduct on the morning of April 3,-1890,
when at Port Ilarford, in command of the
steamer Mexico, Captain Alexander
rescued from drowning an insane man
who had jumped from, one of the ports
into the water between the steamer and
the wharf, where a boat could not go and
where he was in great danger of not only
being drowned but crushed also. Captain
Alexander was about 300 feet from his
vessel at the time, but he was in the
water Inside of a minute and had the man
at the surface, where he held- him against
his will until a rope was lowered and he
was hauled on board. . â¢

YOL'NGSTOWN, Ohio.â The general strike
order will have no effect in Toungstown or the
Mahoning Valley. On July 1. when the old
scale expired, all the mills of the United States
Steel Corporation were closed down and not
a wheel has turned since. In Toungstown the
Steel Hoop Company owns two mills, which
employ 23C0 men. In Ruthers the steel com
pany operates a five-mill plant of 500 men
and In Niles It has a plant employing the same
number of men. In Niles, also, the Tin. Plate
Company operates a plant of 600 men. and In
Warren the Steel Hoop Company's mills are
idle and 500 men are out. .They are all or
ganized and have nearly all secured jobs In
independent mills and outside works. The only
move expected in the valley at an early date
is the strike of the blast furnace men.
MONESSEX, Pa.â The tin mill is running
and the strikers have so far failed in their
efforts to get the non-union men out.

at the Bellalre works of the National Steel

this mill that the Amalgamated Association
would allow to become members, but it Is
thought here that If all the other trades come

WELLSVILLE, Ohio.â The same namber of

In no wise altered. The arrest of H. B. Hen
derson, president of the Wellaville local lodge
of potters, on the charge of riot has deeply in
censed all the pottera In the Ohio Valley.
To-morrow at noon a kitchen will be estab
lished in the mill yard and the non-union men
will be furnished their meals within the mill
yard Instead of having them passed through
the fence as heretofore. Manager Brookman
absolutely refused to admit a reporter to his
office and .will not even tell the number of
mills now working.

It has been the purpose of President

the remaining constituent companies of
the steel corporation time to anticipate
the calling out' of their men. If it was

formal notification this plan was waived

The; call to the men of the three com
panies in the mills of which the Amalga
mated Association is strongest Is expected
to â¢ be generally and promptly responded
to. The movement will, it is expected,
radically suspend operations of the Fed
eral Steel Company and cut off half of the
production .of the National Steel and
plational Tube companies and throw more
than 40.000 men idle.
The attitude of the federation remains
uncertain, and- the president of the Amal
gamated Association is unwilling to dis
cuss it, although support has been prof
fered by officials of the federation. This
has been without concerted action upon
the part of the federation executive board.
Many affiliated bodies of the federation
have annual or long term' contracts with
their employers, and beyond financial and
moral support it is hard to tell how the
federation may go.
Attitude of the Federation.
The - order of President Shaffer is ex
pected to. swell , the number, of idle men
to over 100,000 at the end of the week.
President Shaffer was asked if the order
was intended to go to the union men in
the Carnegie Steel Company. He said that
he answered no questions on that score.
The Amalgamated Association has lodges
in the upper and lower union mills of the
Carnegie Steel Company and a foothold
in the Homestead, Duquesne and Braddon,
mines of the great bulwark of non-union
ism. In conformity to statements that
have been made by President Shaffer be
fore, these men will be expected to join
the strike, as will all Amalgamated men
and sympathizers In all plants of the
United States ' Steel Corporation. The
other companies of the steel corporation
not mentioned by the Amalgamated presi
dent and whose operations the association
will seek to hamper are the American
Steel and Wire Company and the Ameri
can Bridge Company. In the wire com
pany the Amalgamated Association has
only lodges in the Cleveland Rolling Mill
plant and the Joliet Rod Mill plant of the
company. The former Is now idle. -
In the plants of the American Eridge
Company there is no organization of the
men. The outside men handling and
erecting the woik of the American Bridge
Company are organized as the Bridge
and Structural Iron Workers. They are
not affiliated with the American Feder
ation of Labor.

EEDLANDS, Aug. 6.â There was not a
cloud in the sky yesterday at 11 o'clock
over t?ie Santa Ana Canyon watershed,

cloudbursts and until 4 p. m. an unprece
dented falUof rain occurred. Many power
plants were .swept away and small
ranches in the bottom of the big canyon

but the ranchers escaped to the canyon's
walls. Two parties of deer hunters are
missing. Searching parties are looking
for them to-day, but up to 8 o'clock no
trace of them had been discovered. A
Santa Fe passenger agent, C. H. Hobart,
is among those reported missing.

Youngest Retired Admiral.
Rear Admiral Howison is one of the
youngest retired officers of his grade, hav
ing retired October 10, 1S99, when he reach
od the age of 62. During the early part
of the war he served principally on block
ade duty, but later participated in the
Viattle of Mobile Bay as commander of the
V. S. S. Bienvllle. It is an interesting co
incidence that he was in command of the
cruiser Vandalia at Samoa, which later
went down in the hurricane In Apia har
bor while flying the flag of Rear Admiral
Kimberly, who had succeeded him as se
nior officer on the station, and whom, he
now succeeds on this court. He was in
command of the Boston yard during the
Spanish war, and later was commander of
the South Atlantic station. He made the
famous long-distance cruise around Africa
In the Chicago as the last act of his ac
tive career, arriving in New York the
day before Admiral Dewey arrived in the
Olympia. It will be remembered that al
though he outranked Admiral Sampson,
who was in command of the reviewing
fleet at that time, he refrained from as
suming command, but courteously allowed
Admiral Sampson to do the honors upon
that celebrated occasion.
"Witnesses Being Called Home.
A preliminary list of the witnesses who
will be called to appear before the court
has been prepared at the Navy Depart
ment. Some of these officers are on for
eign stations aiid will be obliged to leave
for borne almost immediately in order to
arrive on time.
Admiral Schley has submitted a list of
witnesses, some of whom are on foreign
stations, and these also will be ordered
home.
Among the witnesses desired by Ad
miral Schley is Lieutenant Benjamin W.
Wells, Admiral Schley's flag secretary
throughout the Spanish war. There are
no Spanish officers on Admiral Schley's
list.

lilAYA INDIANS ROUTED
BY THE MEXICAN TROOPS
Many of the Rebellious Redskins
Have Taken Refuge in Erit
ish Honduras.
AUSTIN, Tex., Aug. 6. â A dispatch from
Oaxaca, Mexico, says the campaign of the
Mexican troops against the Maya Indians
in the State of Yucatan is at an end. An
official announcement to that effect has
Just been issued by President Porflrlo
Diaz, who says that the Government has
completely subjugated the rebellious In
dians and that the troops will be with
drawn from the field as rapidly as possi
ble, ilany of the hostile Indians have
taken refuge in British Honduras. Several
military garrisons will be established and
maintained in the Indian country until all
danger of another outbreak has passed.
Mexican teachers are also being sent into
the Indian towns and villages and Govern
ment schools established under the aus
pices of the Government for the educa
tion of the Maya children.

The next step that the Employers' Association
took was to intimidate the Draymen's Associa
tion, to disrupt the Brotherhood of Teamsters
using the argument that If the brotherhood was
disrupted the Packers, Porters and "Warehouse
men would also be disrupted and they then
would turn their attention to other unions as
they came along. "When' the situation grew
critical, that Is when the Draymen's Associa
tion, at the dictation of the Employers' Asso
ciation, decided . to take up Morton's fight
against the Brotherhood of Teamsters then
the merchants who make . up the Employers'
Association changed their tactics a little The-v
represented to their employes that they werft
being treated all right and that they should be
satisfied with the'r situations under existing
conditions.
Move Against the Unions.
The four men In question were reinstated at
their old wage rate and the Incident was closed
for the time being, but from that dateâ last
Marchâ dates the existence of the Employers'
Association, organized to fight, the Packers.
Porters and "Warehousemen in particular and
all trades unions in general. The Employers'
Association, after being organized, lost no time
In' throwing Itself Into the general labor up
heaval. It has interfered in the carriage -work
ers' strike, " the big hardware supply houses
down town refusing supplies to carriage manu
facturers who recognized the union, and it is
safe to say that but for their interference the
carriage workers would have had no trouble. '
The next move of the Employers' Association
was to prevent' their own employes from Join-
Ing the union. Several firms gave their em
ployes to understand that If they Joined the
cnlon they (the firms) could not employ them
any longer. "W. "W. Montague, on Market
street, and Sachs Bros., on Sansome street
gave their men the preference of the union or
their Job. The men remained with their work
and from thone two firms no men Joined the
union. The only reason that more firms did
not follow their example was that about the
time the employers organized the union had
succeeded In getting nearly all the eligible em
ployes of the different business houses to Join
the union.
Â¦ So In the natural/ course of events there
came a time when the men employed as pack
ers and porters and -warehousemen awoke to
their condition, for It will not be denied that
the worktngman has been asleep for a long
time, and hence their organization. As is al
ready known, the big merchants of the city
took a stand against the packers, porters and
warehousemen some months ago and the Em
ployers' Association was organized only after
one of Its foremost membersâ a member of
the firm of Dunham, Carrigan & Haydenâ
had a brush with the union, because the above
mentioned firm took it upon' itself to cut th~
wages of four union members employed there.
Workingmen Were A9leep.
JN. 0'NEIL.Iy, . president of the
Porters', Packers' and Ware
housemen's - Union, has Issued
i*~, the following statement:
The employers In .the mercantile district of
this city â and when I say employers I mean
the wholesale dry poods merchants, the -whole
sale Iron and hardware firms, the wholesale
grocers and produce men. the cereal mill own
ers and managers and the different warehous
ing firmsâ are. with but very few exceptions,
paying comparatively low wages to their em
ployes, notwithstanding the fact that the work,
to a great extent. Is of an exacting nature
and requires. In every instance, that the ap
plicant be fairly educated and possess a cer
tain amount of experience.
When tho big merchants of this city learned
that there was such an organization as tbe
Packers, Porters and Warehousemen In ex
istence, they lost no time in declaring tho
said organization to be a menace to their
business. It 13 not the few extra dollars that
they will bo asked to pay to their underpaid
help that they care about, but it is the fact
that their employes are fully aware that they
are not receiving a just wage, and the mer
chant does not like to admit that he Is paying
wages to hla help that barely provides for the
necessaries' of Hfe. and.it ls'a fact that cannot
be contradicted that many firms in this city
are paying very low wages. Low wages Is
the groove in which the mercantile employers
of this, and of every other city. too. have been
in for the past ten years, and, being In a
groove, and such a groove as it is. it is not to
be wondered at that their progress has not
kept pace with the times. It has ever been
the policy of all employers to force men to
work for them for little or nothing, and with,
as stated above, some few exceptions the em
ploying element of this city are no exception
to tho general, rule.

be towed over from there this morning.
The work of unloading the collier Tellus
at the Dunsmuir bunkers la being accom
plished by two gangs of negroes. The
owners are satisfied with the amount of
work being done.
The ship Royalist is being coaled In the
stream. She will leave for Portland as
soon as her bunkers are filled and load
there with lumber for Taku, China.
The Point Arena will leave to-day for
Point Arena and the Eureka sailed yea
terday. The loading of these two vessels
has been accomplished under great diffi
culties and the owners feel that they ara
extremely fortunate in getting them away
at this time. .
The Umatilla will not be able to start
"on her trip north until Saturday. She
was due to leave last Sunday, but the
freight that she was to carry was handled
so slowly, that it was deemed advisable to
hold her here until all the cargo was
aboard.

A number of vessels that have arrived
here since the Inception of the strike have
succeeded in. getting rid of their freight
by going to Benicla and Redwood City to
unload. The strikers were informed of
this fact yesterday and steps were taken
immediately to put a stop to the practice.
The strike committee of the City Front
Federation was hastily summoned, with
the result that the strike order was
changed so as to include these two bay
ports.

length. Reports were received to the ef

numbering about 530 teamsters, will walk
put this morning. It is expected that this
will seriously cripple the building crafts,
as no mason work can be done on build
ings now in course of construction or to

sand teamsters. It was estimated by a
builder last night that the walkout of
the -teamsters would within a short time
throw 2000 carpenters and allied building
craftsmen out of work.-

In conclusion let it be noted that the mem
bers of the Employers' Association are for the
most part the very employers of the packers,
porters and warehousemen, but that fact docs
not deter its members from making a pro
longed and strong fight and the few who did
remain at work and those who did not have
the manhood to continue the fight the organ
ization is well rid of and the members of the
union are thoroughly glad that the present
crisis has come about, if for no other reason
than to know who are the men.
Believing that the public should know the
exact position of the Packers'. Porters' and
Warehousemen's Union. No. S885. in this flght
and the exact condition of affairs, which
the Employers' Association has been mainly
responsible for, and of the tactics that members
of said Employers' Association are using
against said union, the above statement is
hereby given out.' . J. E. ONEILL.
President Packers'. Porters' and Warehouse
men's Protective Union No. 8885.
But the fight is now on In earnest and those
that are out are fully determined to see the
end and they will stand by their union in this
great struggle, for the cause is good and a
good cause is half the battle.
employers have been more to them than the

The Humboldt Lumber Company has

The Howard Company's engineers have
not been ordered out yet, because the
company has not attempted to discharge
any vessel the crew of which is on strike.
Two engineers and a fireman were called
off the coal bur.kers of the Pacific Coast
Company by Stationary Engineers' Union,
No. 67, because it had been reported the
company would attempt to discharge the
steamer Australia with a non-union steve
dore gang. The company claims to be
ready to fill the engineers' places as soon
as it has men to work on the steamer.

elation. . This request called for a reply deal-
Ing with the contents <Jf the same, which re
ply never came, so the public can readily see
that if the master painters were desirous' of
conferring with the council they had plenty of
time to do so, and it will not now do for Mr.
Black to say that they conferred with No. 73
with bad results. The fact is that the em
ploying painters are themselves to blame and
I hope they will cheerfully take upon them
selves the responsibility of this controversy
now troing on and not try. to place it on the
shoulders of Xo. 73, which is not entitled nor
called upon to bear so heavy a burden.
It is not fair to the material men to say
that they have raised material so high that
with an Increase of a few cents a day in
wages paid the men it prohibits painting. This
I know to be incorrect. AYhen material and
wages are low very little improvements are
going on ; when lumber can be purchased ' for
$9 per thousand and oil and lead are low few
people can be found who care to build, but
when they are high, as Mr. Black says of the
present, building and every other industry is
brisk. Some of Mr. Black's own patrons stated
no later than to-day that his views were not
shared in by them; that if he insisted on his
low wage scale some other, employing painter
would have to do their work. With reference
to his $500 bond I desire to say that it is a
very convenient bond and binds no one only
inasmuch as they desire to be bound by }t.
It was put up when the master painters came
together for the purpose of arranging a sched
ule of prices, below which no man doing a
legitimate trade could figure, and not for the
purpose of fighting the men that do the work
or opposing -a living wage. Thus it will ,be
seen the bond, as I have stated, is a very
convenient one. The Building Trades Council
is in this Instance very sincere and honestly
working to elevate not alone the Journeyman,
but also the employers, and is Â¦willing to co
operate with them at any time for the best
interest of this city and State.
During the past week a number of men
have been rounded up here In Los An
geles and sent by one route or another to
the present scene of Clark's operations.
J. W. Miller of this city has acted in the
capacity, of "enlistment agent." Another
lot has been rounded up In Arizona, and
will congregate at the same center.' Every
man has been asked to go to work at
either driving stakes, packing chain or
handling powder, but at the same time he
has been given to understand that danger
would probably attach to his work, and
his willingness to make a flght If neces
sary has been determined in that way.
When interviewed to-day neither of
Clark's representatives would admit that
a fighting force had been sent to the
desert. ,J. Ross Clark said:
"We have been employing a large num
ber of new men lately, both in the engi
neering anfl other division, and I- think
our interests on the desert are now so
well protected that there Is little danger
of any further action, such as the taking
of the Meadow Valley grade. The men
we have sent in have been picked up here
in Los Angeles and through the country,
and they have been very well provided
with necessary 'camp equipment." "

ANSWERS TO QUERIES.
The new tactics may not prove more successful than
the-old, but they certainly cannot be worse or more
ineffective. Kitchener's huge, army has had no more
chance to catch the nimble Boers than, an elephant
would have, to catch a flea. Ifjias entailed a heavy
expense and it has accomplished nothing beyond cap
turing towns and burning farmhouses. A smaller
army, but with greater mobility, will not cosKso much
and may be- more effective. The reopening^of the
mines will unquestionably be an -important feature in
the new situation. Even if the -Boers do "not come
in to share in the work and the wealth that will result
from mining activity, the community" as a whole will
be benefited. Anything that tends to revive the in
dustries of the Transvaal will be better than the policy
of destruction which has prevailed hitherto.
The new tactics are said to have been suggested by
Lord Milner. ' It is the belief of that statesman
that while little has been gained for the British by
burning the Boer homesteads, much might be gained
by reopening the mines and reviving industry/The
argument is that. if work'' be resumed and money be
once more plentiful in and around Johannesburg a
good many Boers who are now on the warpath would
be inclined to settle down and go to raising N supplies
for the miners. In other words, the Boers are to be
coaxed to come in and make money. Those who do
soare to have the security that the British garrison
can give. Those who do not are to be hunted over
the veldt and, the' hills: by the ,'50,000 horsemen
specially detailed for that purpose.
The withdrawal of so large a body of troops still
leaves Kitchener with an army in excess of 200,000
men. He has 50,000 : men organized and drilled as
mounted infantry, and -with these he intends to camp
on the trail of the Boer commandos and follow them
wherever they go 'or until the last one of them sur
renders or is dispersed and broken. With the re
mainder of his force he will guard the railroads lead
ing to Johannesburg,, and establish in that district a
sufficient army to protect the gold mines and those
who operate them. . .
SOMETHING like 70,000 troops who have been
serving with the. British colors in South Africa
are to be sent home. One story says the :,men
have been recalled by the British War Office because
they are needed in India. Another story says they
are to be sent home by Kitchener because they are
not needed in Africa. As to which of the reports is
true no outsider can decide at this moment. The
one undisputed fact js that the troops are to be sent
away from the seat of war and that new tactics on the
partÂ»of the British a,re*to be employed.
The experience of the army posts seems to be the
same everywhere, though the effect upon the men at
posts in the tropics is likely to be more serious, since
the use of whisk}' there has a very bad effect on the
health of the men. 1 . , >
"I am of the opinion that the temperance people,
with the best of intentions in the world, have made
matters worse. It was one fight where the saloons
and those who oppose them were together when Con
gress abolished the canteen. The whisky men wanted
it done away with so that they could have the'soldiers
to trade with, them and' drink their whisky. They
saw the inevitable resu^. The temperance people did
not see it as a choice between two evils, but, jumping
to the conclusion that they could abolish drinking by
doing away with the post canteen, aided the whisky
men right along." '
Captain Slaker, the commander of the post, says:
"Since the . abolition of the canteen, where the men
could have their beer, right at home, there has been
an increase in the number of arrests. The change has
not been effective to prevent the men from having 1
drink at all. If it did it would be all right, but it has
driven them to seek the saloons,Â»and there they get,
not beer, but whisky. The strong drink is what makes
the trouble.
the men are more likely to get drunk and make
trouble." ;
There is a great increase in drunkenness among the
enlisted men, and the change from the light beer of
the canteen to downtown whisky has had a very de
moralizing effect. The city police are in continual
trouble with the drunken soldiers from payday until
the pay is spent in the deadfalls. The correspondent
says that "the rules are such that after a man has com
pleted his duties, if he is not on guard, he may be
absent until retreat: is sounded, and then until 'lights
out' he is free once more. , This gives the chance
needed to try and drink all the whisky in town, and
the men who carouse will take it. In former times
they had the post canteen, where they would gather
and gossip and drink beer. The beer did not hurt
them much in such surroundings, and the results of
the operation of the system on discipline seemed the
best, when it was considered that the men would
have their liquor. Now when there is no restraint of
the camp around them, when they have whisky in
stead of beer for their beverage, the officers say that
A SPECIAL correspondent of the Oakland En
quirer, writing from Honolulu, says that since
closing the canteen at our army post there the
officers have their hands full.

years of age are to vote without -challenge, as are
all who have borne arms in the service of the United
States, of the Confederate States or of the State of
Virginia. But no veterans who draw salaries or pen
sions of any sort can claim exemption, thus barring
from the benefits of the waiver all the white or colored
ex-soldiers who have taken or wish to take advantage
of the Federal pension laws. His scheme also pro
vides, like that of Alabama, for the establishment of
election boards having authority to admit to registra
tion as voters all "persons of recognized good moral
character."
The Tennessee movement has not yet advanced far
enough to disclose clearly what restrictions will find
favor among the Democrats of that State. In Tennes
see the negroes were never numerically strong enough
to control the politics of the State, and the effort to
exclude them from the polls can be due to nothing
more than a desire to imitate the action of other
Southern States.
In the meantime a number of negroes, of means in
Louisiana have organized. to raise an issue before the
courts which will test the constitutionality of the suf
frage law in that State. From that organization much
good may come. It is quite possible,' that all these
ingenious suffrage schemes which the Southern
leaders have so cunningly thought out and so boldly
pat into effect may burst like a -bubble at the first
?Â¦ouch of the constitution of the United States.

The plan proposed in the Alabama convention pro
vides that all men, white or black, must qualify them
selves for the elective franchise by paying their poll
taxes, proving their ability to read and write and
showing that they have been engaged in lawful occu
pations for twelve months. For the purpose of en
abling illiterate and thriftless whites to escape the re
strictions it is provided further that all old soldiers
or their descendants shall have the right to vote, pro
vided they pay their poll taxes. Boards of registra
tion are tc be appointed in each county to determine
the qualifications of voters and to register them. It
is expected, of course, that the boards will let the
whites in and shut the blacks out, but it is said the
requirement of a lawful occupation is aimed not at
the blacks but at gamblers whom the convention de
sires to deprive of the franchise.
In the Virginia convention Senator Daniel is said
to have devised a plan which differs slightly from that
of any other State. He proposes that a voter must
hereafter possess property valued at $300 at least,
must pay a poll tax of $2 at least six months before
election day and must be able to read the constitution
OF old it was known and said, "Evil communi
cations corrupt good manners," and the
rapidity with which the Mississippi plan of
suppressing negro suffrage is spreading over the
South affords a new proof on a large scale of the truth
of the proverb. Perhaps the Southern manners in
that respect were not very good to begin with, so
that process of corruption has been easy; but under
any circumstances the promptness with which State
after State is following a plan which when first pro
posed was considered unconstitutional and revolu
tionary, is something remarkable. Louisiana, South
Carolina and North Carolina have already suppressed
negro suffrage by law; Alabama and Virginia are at
present working to the same end through constitu
tional conventions, and it is now reported that a
movement has been started in Tennessee to suppress
the colored vote there.
It is to be noted that no two Southern*States have
adopted exactly the same plan in dealing with the
issue. That in itself is not surprising since no one of
them is good enough to incite imitation, but the vari
ation ma}' be due to an intent on the part of the
Southern leaders to try as many plans as possible so
that in case some of them are declared by the Su
preme Court to be unconstitutional, one or more of
them ma}' stand the test and serve as a guide for the

THE investigation into oil rates now
on before the Board of Railroad
Commissioners was resumed yes
terday. The session was fairly In
teresting and an adjournment was taken
until 10 o'clock this morning.
Ex-Judge Sterrett of Los Angeles- has
been 111 for the last two days and there
fore has not taken part In the case. His
assistant. Attorney Norton of Los An
geles, is looking after the interests of the
Santa Fe. Railroad in the absence^ of the,
chief counsel. ". . ". ' . '-,Â¦'
SOUTHERN SUFFRAGE SCHEMES
A CHANCE TO SMILE.
Attorney Short In an interview after
adjournment stated that the case for the
oil men was practically in; that from the
testimony of the witnesses for the railroad
he felt that even a stronger cas* than
expected had been made out; that the
evidence had dicclosed the fact that tho
railroad company was earning on a basis
of J1.000.0CO a year from Kern County
alone, which would probably be doubled
within a year from this time: that their
earnings on the Kern River spur
amounted annually to the original cost
of construction. It. would be -made
perfectly apparent, he said, that the rail
road company was making 'a profit far
out of proportion to reasonable compensa
tion for services rendered, circumstances
considered.
Attorney Short attempted to Introduce
a pamphlet on oil, which contained clip
pings from newspaper articles on the
production along the Southern Pacific
road.- Mr. Hcrrin said he had no objec
tion to its introduction if Mr. Short
would not object to him Introducing cir
culars issued by oil promoters.
W. A. Bissell, assistant traffic manager
of the Santa Fe, was the next witness. He
denied that his line hauled oil from Coal
inga. He said the. Santa. Fe hauled oil
only from Bakersfield; that they com
menced to ship from that point as soon
as the oil fields "were opened; that at the
present time the line was shipping from
fifty to 150 cars a week from the oil fields,
and that the ousiness was constantly on
the increase/
Attorney Short asked Mr. Sproule, who
was recalled, about the McKlttrick line.
He desired to know whether the railroad
company had not received more revenue
from that line than from any similar line
In the oil fields. Mr. Sproule said the Mc-
Kittrick line was in operation long before
the oil developments were thought of. He
added that the Kern River business was
something new and he had doubts as to
Its permanancy. ,

which waa organized to better the condi
tions of the oil market in that section.
He said it was because the oil business

He 'said they were selling oil in Los An
geles at 74 cents, although some wells had

the Bakersfield oil r could be produced at
20 cents per barrel, and that the price was
regulated in Los Angeles by the price of
Bakersfleld oil, with the freight, 51 cents
per barrel, added. Mr. Allen thought the
reduction would not do oil producers any

We have only sought to open the way to the people to repudiate him and all his
works. That way is open now, its traverse is safe for every man. Will the party walk
therein next Tuesday?
Heretofore men have justified snubbing the primary by pleading that when they
appeared they were either not allowed to vote, or their votes were miscounted and tal
lied against the purpose they intended to express. All this was true, and it was a meas
ure of justification for the indifference which threw the city into the hands of bosses, to
be stripped like a sandbagged victim of highwaymen. ' But this is true no longer. The
primary law throws legal sanction around the fountain that is potential with government.
Every citizen is amply protected in the casting of his vote and in having it counted as
truly expressive of his purpose. The people now have it in tfieir power to forever break
up and end bossism and boss rule in San Francisco. If they are indifferent and permit it'
to win at the primaries, they will have no right to complain. If Republicans desire to
canonize Martin Kelly let them accept him as an expression of the best the party can do,
as an incarnation of its spirit, and its ideal leader, and not be ashamed to proclaim him
to the world as the excellent and full flower of San Francisco Republicanism.
We hear and attend the cry that there is no party politics in a city election. Grant
ing tfrat to be true, the city officers are elected by one party or the other. They issue
from ranks trained in one of the great schools of politics. Do Republicans intend to
admit that their politicalschool fails to train men for upright, clean, decent and honest
administration of a city? Do they intend to admit that such training and such men may
be found only in the ranks of the opposition? If they so intend how will they answer a
questioner who asks at what stage of public responsibility Republicans become qualified
for administration?
We think not, and so thinking have been willing to make a fight against a pros
pective rule that will gall the withers of the party if it come to pass. This is a Republi
can city. Every interest of its people combines to make it so.^ Its commerce, prosperity
and progress depend upon the public policies of the Republican party. In its govern
ment it should show the world the best that Republicanism can do in municipal govern
ment. It should, inspire the State with that spirit which must be depended upon in the
important elections next year. Can it do any of these, things by installing Martin 'Kelly
in power?
The Mayor now has the one-man power to remove and appoint If Martin Kelly's
ticket win at the primary and the polls, does any one doubt the realization * of this
plan of the former boss? Does any one doubt that the fire, police, schools and public
works â¢will be given Qver to Martin Kelly, farmed out by him to use as he pleases? Un
fortunately, he is at present figuring as a Republican boss. 'He desires to usurp the or
ganized power of the part]/, which is composed largely of the- intelligent, upright and public
spirited men of the city. Do they accept him as an outward- and visible sign and moral
embodiment of the virtue and genius of the Republican party? Do they expect to point
to him with pride as their party leader? ' ( .
Whenever this has been the case the business men, the citizens who /furnish the
capital* and enterprise that make the city, have gone about: the, streets Meploring condi
tions that exist solely by their permission. The municipal government originates in 'the
primary* That is the fountain head, and the stream that flows irom it will rise no higher
than its source. If the primaries are given over to Martin Kelly, and result in the elec
tion of a ticket, we will have a Martin Kelly government, and those who liave '..by- indif
ference permitted it will have no right to complain of the result. The ' outside world
will have a perfect right to say that San Francisco wants that kind of a. government,
and the finger of scorn will be pointed not at the government, but at the people of the
city. Let us be under no illusions in the matter. The charter concentrates great power
in the Mayor. That office is the prize sought by the political looters. It is the key that
unlocks the municipal treasure house to the spoilsman. The most skillful and astute boss
that ever ruled this city earnestly desired the affirmance of the right of Judge Wallace
to remove the old Board- of Supervisors. He said: "I wish that power to remove put
in. the hands of one man, one officer. Then. I will elect that officer/and if a Board of
Supervisors get stiff-necked I will oust them and appoint their successors, who will not
be so stiff." ' .
San Francisco has been boss ridden, and swarms of parasites have stung and bled
hefi She has suffered, morally and materially, from the control of men whose only in
terest in her was what profit they could get through a political control that' was sought
for personal and not public ends. - ~
Â¦ - . Â¦ ..-'Â¦â. '.Â¦Â¦'Â¦Â¦Â¦ ...Â¦Â¦Â¦Â¦- Â¦Â»Â» ... -., Â¦ Â¦ Â¦ . - . Â¦ ... ;..
THE contest at the primaries next Tuesday is for the uplifting of 'the politics of
this cltyy and putting- its public affairs in clean hands. The present administra
tion of the city 'has been repudiated by its own party and the party organ. .While
that may. not be significant, it is true that the administration has proved far from
satisfactory to the people, and those who have the most at stake in the good name and
prosperity of the city hope to see its affairs committed to more skillful and unselfish men.

Â¦When any of the German fleet made port
he had to hire a boat to reach them, but

fitted with an eight-horse power Union
gas engine and on her trial trip beat Joe
Kane's new boat and Clark's flier. Catter
mole says he is now open to race any
thirty-two-foot launch in the bay. Â¦

Shipping Intelligence.
â¢ A cable from South America to the
Merchants Exchange states that a con
siderable portion of the Kosmos liner
Tanls' cargo will be saved. She was on
her way from San Francisco for Ham
burg when she went ashore near the
mouth of the River Platte and it was
then reported that vessel and cargo would
be a total loss. Yesterday's information
states that more than 300 tons of the
cargo has been salved.
Salvage From the Tanis.
Captain Henry Bingham, the well
known stevedore, is lying seriously
Injured at the .Waldeck Sanitarium. Mon
day last he started to catch the traln v at
Mill Valley and, stumbling over a rail
road tie, fell and broke his leg. More
than a year ago he was playing with
some children at Mill Valley and fell over
a tent rope and broke his leg. It was this
same limb he broke Monday, and this
time it is so aerious that it is feared the
leg may have to be amputated to save his
life. Captain Bingham lives at Mill
Valley.
Captain Henry Bingham Injured.
A new four-masted barkentine, now
building on Puget Sound for the Charles
Nelson Company, will be ready to go into
commission next October. She will be 200
feet long, 42 feet beam. 16 feet 6 inches
deep and will carry 1,300,000 feet of lum
ber She will be christened Thomas P.
Emigh and will probably make her first
vovage to Australia with a load of lum
ber The new barkentine will be a very
handsome vessel and will prove a valu
able addition to the Nelson fleet.
New Vessel for Australian Trade.
The British bark Melanope arrived from
Newcastle, Australia, yesterday after a
good run of 66 days. On the 1st inst. she
was caught in a heavy gale, during which
the wind blew from all points of the com
pass. Then the vessel ran Into a dead calm
and a dense fog. Early on the morning
of Monday last the North Farallon
loomed up less than 200 yards away.
Luckily there was but little way on the
vessel and the crew, by bracing the yards
up sharp, managed to get her away from
the danger point. â¢
The Melanope has completed her first
round trip for her new owners, J. J. Moore
& Co. When she last came here the cap
tain had died at Panama. The woman
who passed as his wife had died soon aft
er a start was made for San Francisco
and the bark arrived here in charge of
the mate. There was a lot of litigation
over both vessel and cargo and finally
both were sold at public auction, J. J.
Moore being the purchaser.
on North Farallon.
British Bark Melanope Nearly Go3S
The Albatross left Seattle the latter part
of May and since that time has been
cruising around the spawning grounds and
Behm Canal, Tongas Narrows. Cross
Sound, Icy Straits. Yakutat, Dry Bay and
Prince Williams Sound have been inves
tigated thoroughly.
SEATTLE, Aug. 6.â The Fish Commis
sion ship Albatross arrived at Sitka List
Tuesday flying a yellow flag. She had a.
mild case of smallpox in her crew. Short
ly after coming to anchor she was board
ed by Dr. J. C. Koosher, who has been re
cently ar pointed health inspector, and was
quarantined. The ship was sent to Naka
wasina.=ky Bay for isolation, where she
will stay for twenty-one days.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time of
day: the third time column gives the last tide
of the day; except when there are but three
tides, as sometimes occurs. The heights given
are In addition to the soundings on the United
States Coast Survey charts, except when a
minus (â ) sign precedes the height, and then
the number given is subtracted from the depth
given by the charts. The plane of reference is
the mean of the lower low waters.

Chairman McMillan of the steering
committee said to-night that he still felt
confident of the outcome. He claimed that
the tactics pursued by the Cummins fac
tion in forcing the break from Herriott
Â¦would prove a boomerang and would cost
Cummins more than he had gained.
"We shall certainly control the organ
ization of the convention," he said.
"There is no longer any doubt of that,
and the report of the committee on cre
dentials will be sustained by the conven
tion. After we have shown that we are
in control of the convention we can agree
upon whom shall be nominated."
The defection of Herrlott's men is con
sidered to practically settle the nomina
tion in favor of Cummins. His opponents
conceded to him early in the evening T5C
votes on the first ballot, while he claimed
791. The break to-night, even by the ad
mission of his opponents, gives him 828
on the second ballot, while according to
his own estimate he will have at least S47
on the first ballot and a total of 869 on the
second, while but 821 votes are necessary
to a choice.

DELEGATES DESEB.T HEBBIOTT.
"To the cry that this is a white man's
State," said ho, "we answer that the
negro bogey will scare no man. It is dis
pusting but humorous: it is a sham and
fraud: it is without force. The sugges
tion that more than SO per cent of the
white population of the State is in jeop
ardy from any standpoint from the less
than 20 per cent of negro population is an
insult to a brave and intelligent people,
who are Justly proud of the glory of their
State. There has never been a time when
there has not been a white man's govern
ment in Maryland, and as the percentage
-of colored people has steadily decreased
in the State each decade since 1810, when
it was 3S.22, to the present time, when it
is but 17.18 per cent, the puerility and
absurdity of crying aloud a white man's
government Is apparent."
Both nominations were made by accla
mation, no other names coming before the
convention. Interest, therefore, centered
mainly in the platform and the speeches,
all of which teemed with denunciation of
Democracy in general and former Senator
Gorman in particular. The "whites
supremacy" Issue raised . bv the Demo
crats in the platform adopted by them at
last week's convention was ignored in the
declaration upon which the Republican
candidates will stand, but it came in for
unfavorable mention in the speeches.
Phillips Lee Goldsborough, chairman of
the Republican State Central Committee,
Indicated clearly- the course to be pursued
by his party <n the coming fight with
regard to this issue in his opening address
to the convention.

Contract for a New Corporation.
A suit involving the sale of the Clare
mont, University and Ferries Railroad
Company of Berkeley to the Transit Com
pany of Oakland was , filed yesterday by
C. J. Bruschke against Alvinza Hayward.
Bruschke demands $100,000 damages for al
leged breach of contract.
Bruschke alleges "that on May .8 last
Hayward contracted to sell him 401 shared
of the capital stock of the railroad com
pany. A payment of $500 was made on the
contract, Bruschke avers, the further con
sideration of $3000 being . due on July 8.
On that date Bruschke alleges he was
ready anfl willing to pay the sum named
for the stock, which would give him con
trol of the road, but Hayward refused to
deliver the same.
Attorney Bert Schlesinger, who with
Judge Denson and:.William T. Hubbard
represents the plaintiff, stated last even-
Ing that the road was worth $100,000 . to
Bruschke, who is establishing a furniture
factory in Berkeley. After the first $500
was paid on the agreed price of $3500 Mr
Schlesinger says the Transit Company of
fered Hayward as high as $50,000 for the
road,- being anxious to use it as a link in
the belt line, and Hayward accepted the
company's offer. v . â¢
Bruschke now sues to recover what he
considers his actual loss.
G. J. Bruschke Institutes Action In
volving Sale of Berkeley Road
to Oakland Company.

Contrary to the wishes of the attorneys,
the divorce suit instituted by Abraham
Tuchler against Mary L. Tuchler was
tried with "open doors" yesterday. The
attorneys stated that the evidence in the
case was of a nature which should be
kept from the public. Judge Dunne stat
ed, however, that he wished the bar to
understand that in no case hereafter
would he order the papers in any case
over which he had jurisdiction sealed, no'
would he hear any oases behind closed
doors. If people go into court, he said,
the public is entitled to know. the cause
of and the facts surrounding their diB
pute. The attorneys forthwith proceeded,
and Tuchler was granted a divorce from
his wife on statutory gTounds.

State Department. "Washington, Aug. 6.
His Imperial and Royal Majesty, Wllhelm II,
German Emperor, Berlin: I learn with deep
sorrow of the death of "your Majesty's beloved
mother, the Dowager Empress and Queen
Frederick. Her noble qualities have endeared
her memories to the American people, in whose
-name and In my own I tender to your Majesty
heartfelt condolence.
WILLIAM McKINLEY.
* Emperor "William has received a private
telegram of 'sympathy from the Pope.
"WASHINGTON, Aug. 6.â President Mc-
Kinley has sent the following message to
Emperor "William of Germany on the
death of his mother, the Dowager Em
press Frederick: '- . -
On Thursday the royal family will attend
the funeral -service in the castle, at which
the Bishop of Ripon, who was summoned by
the Empress, will officiate. ,Â¦ Only the immedi
ate family will be present.
Saturday evening the coffin will be escorted
from Friederlchshoff to the Protestant church
in Cronberg by a torchlight procession fol
lowed by the royal family on foot.
Sunday afternoon a funeral service will -be
held in the presence of the family of the Em
press, her household and a 'few of her friends
and other privileged persons.
It is expected here that King Edward will
be present. â¢ Â¦ Â¦
The royal family will go to Potsdam Sunday
evening and the body will be taken there Mon
day. -
The funeral . service in the mausoleum at
Friedenskirche, Potsdam, . will be held Tues
day. As it was the Empress' wish that there
should be no state ceremony, the service will
not be attended by all the German royal , per
sonages. It will be as simple as possible.
Emperor "William had a long interview
with Count von Bulow to-day. He .drove
from Homburg/to Friederichoff, and soon
after his arrival there the following offi
cial announcement regarding the funeral
arrangements was made:
CRONBERG, Aug. 6.â In accordance
with the expressed wishes of s the late
Empress Dowager Frederick, the funeral
services will bo as simple as possible un
der the circumstances. . The body will re
main upon the bed where she died until
ready for the coffin.' ,The roses In which
It Is almost buried are from her private
garden, many of the blooms being from
bushes cultivated with her own hands.
No one will be allowed to view the body
except the members of "the Immediate
family and household.
No One Except Members of
the Family .Will View

Dowager "Will Be

Sheep Skins, Influenced by the Improved con
dition of the Wool market, are in better de
mand and selling freely.
Wet Salted Hides are in good demand. Â¦ and
locally there is every indication for a steady
market. There was a slight advance during
the month of July, but the higher figures could
not be maintained and prices were reduced to
the quotations of our last prices current.
Dry Hides and Calf are wanted and prices
are -well maintained In the local market.
Horse Hide market Is stronger, and only
limited quantities are being received.
Deer Skins at the present prices are on good
trading basis, and sell freely.
The Wool market has greatly improved and
a number of good sized sales have been made.
Wool Is well cleaned up and firm, and dealers
say that they could sell a good deal more if It
were here.
Hops are steady, and the California crop is
said to be turning out lighter than expected.
Quotations for new goods are nominal, though
buyers would take hold at 13%c.

NEW YORK. Aug. 6.â General conditions, in- i
dependent of the strike situation, received |
more consideration to-day than for some time (
and were reflected In higher prices for securi
ties. The absence during the greater part of
the day of any sensational strike news, com
bined with the support which the United
States Steel stocks received after their opening
weakness, also aided the improvement. These
conditions underwent a radical change/. In the
final dealings when a report was current that
orders Tor a general strike of the steel workers
would be issued to-night. The professional
operators promptly put out large short lines
and brought about declines of from l.to about
3 points In the most prominent stocks. The
market in detail practically showed a pretty
steady improvement from the outset, the only
real setbacks which were forced by realiza
tions being quickly overcome by a good de
mand for both accounts and evidences of In-
Side support. London's attitude was regarded
with some interest, but arbitrage operations
proved small and were without Influence. News
front the crop regions was more encouraging
and was supplemented by the weekly Govern
ment crop report, which indicated Improved
conditions at important points. The outlook
for a large crop of epring and winter wheat,
in lddltion to some exceptional railroad earn
ings, also helped to sustain prices. Support
was noticeable for stocks which were especially
weak yesterday, these including the Eastern
trunk lines. Southwestern*, Tractions and Pa
cifies, where improvements had been made .of
fron: 1 to 3% points. Sugar felt an early re
sumption of yesterday's weakness, but after
losing a point sold up four. All these gains
were considerably reduced during the late Bell-
Ing movement and net gains were limited to
fractions. Trading in the steel stocks was
comparatively light, and there were not many
large individual blocks. But 6000 shares were
dealt, in at the opening at a gain of % per
cent. The stock soon yielded''% below yes
terday's close, but later on got % above and
ended unchanged for the day. Pressure was
noted against the preferred right from the
Etart. but after yielding % it got % above
yesterday's final figures and ended with a
loss of %. Money rates ruled easy to-day
and the chief feature of this department was
the debit of $1,392,198 by the sub-treasury at
the clearing-house this morning on account of
, drafts drawn against gold deposited at Pa
cific Coast points en route from the Klondike.
Foreign exchange rates were firmer to-day, de
mand bills bringing $4 87%, with the demand
only moderate.
Railroad bonds were quiet to-day and the
price movement was Irregular. Total sales
Â»1.07Â£,0C0.
The closing of the stock market was â¢ weak,
with business In increasing volume. United
States bonds were all unchanged on the last
call.

Dried Fruits, Nuts and Raisins.
Almost everything under this, head is in
much better shape than a fortnight ago. The
recent remarkable sales of Prunes and Raisins
have pretty well ' cleaned up the market for
these two descriptions, and given prices a
much firmer tone. The Prune crop will be
very light everywhere, and the official can
vass of Sonoma County by the association
shows only 25 or 30 per cent of a crop there.
The Santa Clara Valley is also very light.
The demand for Apples. Peaches and Apri
cots continues heavy at firm prices, and. Pears
promise to open high. There is no talk ot
Plums or new Prunes yet. Nuts are very firm
and new Almonds are bringing much higher
prices than expected, as already mentioned.
Honey is rather quiet, but prices are steady.

OATSâ There is a lively demand for the
drought-stricken regions of the West and if
the railroads will make a rate large shipments
overland from this State will result. The situ
ation is explained under the head of Barley.
The market rules firm and sellers make no
concessions. The demand in the city market is
not very brisk, but as, previously mentioned
the Government is in the market for 4000 tons
of old Oats for Manila, and this, with the
Western demand mentioned above and the
speculative inquiry already spoken of, tends to
render the tone quite . firm. Prices are un
changed at 95c@$l 02% for Black and 97%c@
$1 15 per ctl for Red.

BARLEY â A good demand, has sprung up for
the drought districts in the West.- Free ship
ments overland have already been made from
the southern part of the State and dealers say
that if the railroads will make a rate at which
they can ship there will be a very large move
ment for this account from the central and
northern districts. They are now in consulta
tion with the railroad companies. The market
is steady under this possible new demand, but
prices show no advance. The local market for
Feed continues very quiet.

LOS ANGELES SUMMARY-Heavy rain in
eastern mountains and valleys Sunday. Apri
cot drying about over; yield better than was
expected. Early peaches are ripening rapidly.
EUREKA SUMMARYâ Fogs were of tome
benefit to growing crops, but on high lands
vegetation is parched and needs rain badly.
Apples are ripening rapidly; some are on the
market. Reapers arc at work in the Â«rain
fieWs.
The treather has been generally clear and
hot durinp the week, with light showers In
com 1 ? sections and fogs along the coast. In
some places orange trees are putting out a
fiecend ret of blossoms and the 'small oranges
are tailing; there will be a light crop in the

cropping badly in SMne orchards In Orange
County Peaches will yield a fair crop in
E'jme sections. Grapes are in excellent con

SOUTHERN CALIFORNIA
Very warm and scrr.ewhat sultry weather
prevailed during the week, with a light sprinkle
of rain in most sections Wednesday, too light
to do damage. The grain harvest is progress
ing rarid!j", and <n most sections is nearly
or quite completed. Deciduous fruits are ripen
ins' end in most sections the crop is good ana
the quality excellent. Peaches and pears are
being handled In large quantities by the can
.neries. Large shipments of green fruit are be
ing made to Eastern points and of melons to
the Kortlnrcst. Grapes are making good prog
ress and early varieties are being marketed.
Alfalfa iE doing well and In some localities the
fourth crop is being harvested. Stock of all
kinds are in tood condition.-
SAN JOAQUIX VALLEY.
Continued warm weather during the week
has been favorable for crops. Light showers
of rain have fallen in some sections and fogs
have prevailed along the coast. Grain harvest
and thrashing are progressing rapidly. Wheat
and barley are cf excellent quality and in
some rlaces the yield is very heavy. Corn,
heps and beans are doing well and will yield
fair crops, while in other places the yield will
be very light. Fruit shipments have been
eeriously interfered with during the week and
there will probably be a considerable loss to
crchardir-te. Citrus iruits are in good con
dition.
AND BAY SECTIONS
High temperatures have continued during the
week. Light rain has fallen In the foothills;
in some places the precipitation was sufficient
to extinguish the brush and gra^s fires, but
caused no injury to crops. The extremely hot
weather has caused a rapid growth of hops
and they will mature earlier than usual. So
far as reported there has been no injury to
prapes by the heat, probably owing to the
heavy foliage of the vines. Heavy shipment*
of fruit are being made from Sacramento.
Citrus fruits are in excellent condition and
will probably yield heavy crops. Harvesting
Is still in progress anJ will continue for about
two weeks in som; sections. Wheat and bar
ley are yielding large crops and the quality of
the grain ii- excellent. The hay crop Is light
in portions of Butte County.
SACRAMENTO VALLEY.
Grain harvest is i-tlll in progress, though
rapid'.v drawing to a close in most sections.
Wheat, barley and rye are yielding excellent
crops in all sections except Southern Califor
nia, and fair crops are being gathered in some
jiarts of the south. The fourth crop of alfalfa
is being cut in portions of the San Joaquin
Valley. Hops have made very rapid grrowth
curing the hot weather and will mature con
siderably earlier than usual; there will be a
good crop. Corn, beets and beans have also
advanced rapidly and good crops are predicted.
Irrigation water is getting low in some sec
tions, but is still abundant in the San Joaquin
Valley.
Conditions were very favorable for fruit dry
ing during the week. Deciduous fruits are
generally of good quality, but the yield is
much below average. Orchardists will suffer
a considerable loss through lack of transpor
tation facilities. Grapes are in good condition
in r.early all sections, but it is reported that
the yield will fall below average. Citrus fruits
er.d walr.ute are in good condition in most
Places
Nearlv normal weather conditions have pre
vailed in all parts of, the State during the
vetk. The temperature has continued high in
the interior, and warm weather, with oc
casional fogs, has prevailed along the coast.
L:^t showers have fallen in some sections,
mostly in the foothill districts.

DECIDUOUS FRUITS.
Peaches In bulk for canning account are very
firm, and clings bring $40 . without difficulty.
Bartlett Pears are easy at the moment, but
cannot be called weak. They are not In large
supply anywhere this year, and the usual mid
summer glut in this market has not yet ap
peared. Plums and Prunes continue firm at
the ; good prices, and almost anything will
bring $20 per ton in bulk. Figs continue very
scarce. Apricots are going out.
Cantaloupes and Nutmegs continue In liberal
supply and weak at the decline already noted.
There is a very fair demand for Watermelons,
supplies of which are not excessive.

SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
CHICAGO, Aug. 6.â While the Liverpool 'mar- |
ket showed -some advances -it was disappoint- <
Ing when compared .with the bulge in wheat
here yesterday. In addition it was- believed
the recent heavy movement to the other side
would show a decrease when the seaports were
heard -from. These . considerations in a re
stricted market had their natural effect at the
beginning of business, September opening
Vb@ ] /4c under yesterday at,70%@70%c- On cov
ering a small line of short wheat the price
was run up to 70?sC^within- five minutes of the
opening. For- some time .thereafter the tone
was weak, sympathy with corn and the heavy
receipts being depressing factors. Â¦ Selling was
oh an extremely moderate scale, but prices did
not steady until September was quoted at
69%c. A sharp rally to 70%c followed on re
ports of heavy export Â¦ sales, but the absence
of confirmation, of - these reports , resulted In
an equally sharp decline to. 70c and the close
found the market weak, September %@%c ua
der yesterday at IQQ'VAc.
Nervousness caused a rather narrow flutter-
Ing of prices in Â¦ the corn market, but while
messages from . the West were of the usual
gloomy tenor the feeling that all drought dam
ages : had been liberally discounted was the
ruling factor in the market. - The volume of
trade was small. September corn . closed lc
under yesterday at 56%c.
With purely local conditions governing sen
timent the oats market ruled dull and nar
row. September closed %c lower at 34%c.
Provisions . for September delivery ruled dull
and easy in sympathy with lower hog prices
and also with corn. Pork closed 20c lower,
lard 12%c down and ribs 10@12c lower.

The Butter market maintains the advance
with which the week opened, but the movement
is not brisk and there is sufficient here to meet
all current requirements.
Cheese continues firm with moderate sup
plies. .
Eggs are slower since prices were so sharply
marked up, but dealers report sales at full
figures. Stocks are lighter than they have been.

cases of Eggs. 3000 pounds of California Cheese.

0the dead. The festival usually begins with
the sound of chains being dragged over
the chairs and furniture. After about an
hour of this the tumult seems to change,
then resembles that produced when people
break up , housekeeping, accompanied by
the dull thud of falling timbers and crash
ing glass. Then follow in quick succession
until daylight a series of blood-curdling
moans, gramaphone selections and cornet
solos, above which can be heard the omin
ous rush through the air of the winged
spooks. It is all a great strain on Mr. Mc-
Inerney's nervous economy.
That the spooks are trying to break him
up in business Mr. Mclnerney does not
doubt. He has seen himself go to bed
with a great pile of bill-posters alongside
of him, but in the morning they had all
disappeared. For this reason he has been
charged by his employers with destroying
the bills, no results having followed their
outlay Jn -advertising. He -hates to have
his honesty impugned and his slumbers
destroyed, hence the arrest.
