Detective Ed Gibson arrested Leong
Gong,- alias Leong Sun. a notorious high
binder, last evening and charged him with
the murder of Gee Chong, who -. was slain
in Baker alley last Thursday night. His
accomplice will be arrested to-day.
Chinese Murderer Arrested.
Auditor Wells requested his attorneys.
Lloyd & Wood, yesterday to furnish him
with an opinion regarding the legality- of
the tax levy for last year. The levy in
cluded a tax for interest and sinking funds
on park, school, sewer and hospital bonds,
which were declared invalid by the Su
preme Court. "Wells is inclined to the be
lief that, the whole levy may be invali
dated, because of the illegal tax for the
bonds. He. thinks that when property is
sold to the State for non-payment of taxes
the owners thereof will have good legal
ground for resisting the sale. He is about
to file his report with the State Controller
and will embody therein the opinion of
his -attorneys. ~ ..
Legality of Tax Levy Questioned.
Fire on Hay Wharf.
Shortly after 11 o'clock last night fire
broke out on the wharf at Third and' Kins
streets in hay that had been baled for
shipment. Before the flames were sub
dued the damage had amounted to about
$800. The fire-boat Governor Irwin was
tied to the wharf and did good service ln>
extinguishing the fire.
Ar contest Is on over the guardianship ot
the limited estate of Catherine Ryan,
now an inmate of Napa Asylum. Mrs.
Ryan was divorced two years ago from
John Ryan, who is now in Australia." '
After her reparation from her hus
band. Mrs. Ryan began to fail mental
ly. A few days ago she was committed
to the asylum. Following Mrs. Ryan's
commitment. Mrs. John Pettee. president
of the Universal Service Society, applied
for letters of guardianship upon her
estate, which consists of about J60 in bank
and an income of $10 a week, from rela
tives in England.
An opposition to Mrs. Pettee's petition
was filed by Miss S. L. Morris. Mis9
Morris asserts that prior to her com
mitment Mrs. Ryan, who felt that she
was failing mentally, asked her to care
for her son and for herself if anything
happened to her. Miss Morris' assertion
is corroborated by Mrs. Ryan's son.
Arthur I* Hartridge, and by a Miss "Whit
ney, a friend of the unfortunate woman.
The case was called for hearing by Judge
Dunne yesterday, but was continued on
the motion of counsel until a week from
Thursday.
Mrs. Jonn Pettee and Hiss S. L. Mor-
ris Seek Control of Insane
Woman' Estate.

Charles Molter, who was restored to
capacity on the 5th Inst., filed suit yes
terday- charging, his wife. Lena Molter,
with having entered into a conspiracy
with one Harry Blews to defraud the
plaintiff out of his property. Molter
alleges that when he married In Is. 9 he
had $600 and rhewas possessed of $350.
Through thrift and industry, he say3, he
accumulated sufficient money to purchase
the properties' at 1110 Mission street and
607 Jessie street.
On August 10. 1S99. Molter continues, hia
wife caused him to be adjudged insane
and secured his commitment to the Napa
Asyluir. for the purpose of defrauding
him of his property. Moiter claims that
after his- commitment his wife disposed
of a portion of his property and invested
the proceeds in a cigar store at 140 First
street.
After possessing herself of the cigar
store. Molter says his wife entered into a
conspiracy with Blewa to dispose of the
rest of his estate. Molter asks that the
court restrain his wife and Blew:* and
adjudge that Mrs. Molter holds the prop
erty in question in trust for his use. and

F. Beck was formerly the dairyman at the
State Hospital for the Insane and resided with
his wife on North Ophir street. In July ha
went to San Francisco, intending later to take
a trip East. A few days ago he sent his wife
a small money order and she went down to
Join him. She Is probably In Alameda, where
she has relatives. Beck was an industrious
man and his friends here believe he must
have met with foul play. He had some money.
There was also found on the body a
picture of the deceased dressed as a sol
dier. -
East Sunday Lorenz found a half
emptied box of rough on rats about 200
feet from where the body was discov
ered, also a half-filled bottle of whisky
containing a portion of the deadly poi
son. From his statement the Coroner
came to the conclusion that Beck, who,
it was said, lived in Stockton, had com
mitted suicide.
The following dispatch from Stockton
gives facts as to Beck's character that
do not coincide with the suicide theory:
The body was that of a person about 40
years of age. It "was clothed In a blue
serge suit and a black soft hat. On each
cuff was the name "F. Beck." In one of
the pockets was found a money order re
ceipt showing that the man had drawn
$7 from the Stockton Postofflce on July
19 last.
George Lorenz, "who is employed at the
Sutro Baths, last evening found the body
of a middle-aged man near Sutro Heights.
He at once notified the Coroner.
Friends Think He Was Mur
dered, Though Suicide
Is Probable.

Meets Death in a
Lonely Spot.

DENVER. Aug. 6.â A special to the
Xews from Santa. Fe, N. M. t says: Farm
crops on. both the east and west slopes
of the Glorieta range have been destroyed
by the flood. Fences were washed away
and along Torreon Creek the corn and
oai fields are strewn with driftwood piled
five feet high. Between Glorieta and
Lamy the public highway is practically
obliterated. Just above Canoncito the
Galisteo's swift current was eighty feet
wide and twenty feet deep. At numerous
points the railroad track is undermined
for hundreds of feet. The heavy piling
and timber guards that have protected
the roads for. years in the canyon half a
mile east of Canoncito are destroyed.
In some places the rails and ties for 150
feet are suspended in midair twenty feet
above what is now tj^ creek bed. The
two sixty-foot spans of the steel bridge
at Canoncito were carried down stream
sixty feet and now lie bottom-side up
half buried in sand. The famous well at
Canoncito was entirely filled with gravel
and wash. From all indiciat'ions it will
require at least five days to repair tho
roadbed between the top of the mountain
and Lamy. Â¦';â¢â¢Â¦;: ~y - ; â¢/â¢;Â¦,*â¢. v^*;

0AKLAND, Aug. 6.â A brilliant so
cial event was the wedding thi3
evening of Miss Elizabeth Stan
ford Gage of this city and Wil
liam H. Richardson Jr. of Austin,
Tex." The bride is the youngest daughter
of Stephen. T. Gage and one of . the most
popular members of the exclusive set.
.The invitation list was a long one and
scores of society people returned during
the last' few days from the summer re
sorts to be present at the wedding. Â¦
Preparations for the event had been
made on an elaborate scale at the Gage
home on Harrison street, where the cere
mony and reception took place, and the
result was striking and artistic. Green
and white prevailed, an occasional sug
gestion .of color being given with pink
roses and sweet peas. In the embowered
bay-window in the drawing-room a large
white wedding bell hung, beneath which
the young couple knelt during the mar
riage service. The Rev. Robert Ritchie,
rector of St. Paul's Episcopal Church,
officiated. " ' '
While these demonstrations are going
on, which is mostly all night, of course,
there is more or less noise, and to expect
Mr. Mclnerney to sleep is asking too
much. Sometimes there is. .such . a . large
quantity of noise that Mr. Mclnerney says
he would not be surprised if it awakened
Mrs. Rcgers holds seances nightly in her
apartments, attended often by the entire
colored population. She gives everybody
a chance to speak face to face with
friends in the spirit form; gives names,
facts, figures and luck; honest predictions
in love affairs, shows picture of future
husband or wife, reunites the separated,
holds the great Egyptian secret, and suc
ceeds where all others fair.
Rogers, obese and a decided brunette.
Every time she performs an evolution,
Mclnerney says, he thinks an earthquake
Is approaching on horseback. The Mcln
erneys and the Rogerses never did get
along well together and Police Court an
nals reveal that they have each other ar
rested regularly once a week.

RESULTS ON EASTERN TRACKS.
MRS. DELLA ROGERS. A COLORED MEDIUM, HAS BEEN ARRESTED FOR DISTURBING THE SLUMBERS OF
ANGUS McINERNEY. WHO SAYS HIS REPUTATION HAS SUFFERED THROUGH THE OPERATIONS OF
SPOOKS REGULARLY EMPLOYED BY THE ACCUSED.
It" is expected that the gunboat Machals
will start in a few days, . and it would
not be surprising should Rear Admiral
Casey be directed to send one of the ships
of his command to Panama. Thus the
United States will have on both sides of
the isthmus warships which will be able
to protect not only the terminus of the
railroad, but to land men for the purpose
of traveling on trains and of preventing
an interruption of traffic. ,
Officials of Colombia in this city are
rather glad the incident has occurred.
The moral effect of the intervention by
the United States in isthmian affairs will
be great. Â¦Â¦'Â¦r-.S'-'V-;-. - .
"Liberals detained for one hour a pas
senger train at Matachin this morning.
Captured some Government officials. No
looting, no damage."
The treaty in force between the United
States and Colombia imposes upon this
Government the duty of efficaciously and
positively preserving perfect neutrality of
the isthmus, "with the view that free
transit from one to the other sea may not
be interrupted or embarrassed." In or
der to maintain free communication it is
necessary that an armed force should be
sent to Colombia.
CALL" BUREAU, 1406 " Q STREET,
N. W., WASHINGTON, Aug. 6.â An in r
terruption of transit across the isthmus,
which the American Consul in. Colon said
last week was threatened by operations
of the Colombian guerrillas, has occurred
and it will now become necessary for the
United States to take action to prevent Its
recurrence. The interruption was short,
only one hour, yet it establishes a prece
dent which this Government cannot afford
to overlook. The facts of the incident
were communicated by Consul General
Gudger-in Panama in this dispatch to-day
to the State Department: V
Bpecial Dispatch to, The Call.
Holding Up of a Train by
Guerrillas Gives a Prece-:
dent for Action.
To Prevent Further In
terruption of Transit
Across Isthmus.

CHICAGO. Aug. 6.â Judge TVilliam Cecil
Price, United States Treasurer under
President Buchanan, died here to-day at
the home of his son-in-law, "William S.
Newberry. aged S6 years. _.__,. , r
Judge Price settled in Springfield, Mo.,
in the early 30s. An ardent believer in
slavery, he became prominent in the ranks
of the' Democratic party and when Buch
anan was elected he was given the keys
cf the Treasury. '
When Buchanan ordered Major Anaer-
Fon to reinforce Fort Sumter Judge Price
believed the South was being betrayed and
resigned his office in anger. He was with
General Price at the battle of Pea Ridge
and was captured by the Federals and
confined in the prison at Alton, 111., until
September. 1SC2. when he was exchanged.
Judge Price has been in ill-health for
some time owing to his advanced age and
the end was not unexpected. The body
will be taken to Madison, Wls., for inter
ment Thursday.

The Citizen Hook and Ladder Com
pany was organized September 16, 1876. It
has had among Its members most of Ala
meda's prominent citizens, including
Judge John Ellsworth, Fritz Boehmer and
the late John Leise.
The action of the hook and ladder com
pany leaves only four volunteer hose com
panies in the department. . The "hose
wagons manned by these companies all
have paid drivers and in case of their
withdrawal there will be only one man to
each. A hook and ladder company, is ab
solutely necessary. Â¦ â¢â¢ Â¦
The City Trustees were surprised at the
document and even Chief Krauth did not
know it was coming. President Forderer
said' it meant that the city would soon
be compelled to organize a paid depart
ment, but he could not see where the
money is coming from at present.
. Resolved, That we. the members of Citizen
Hook and Ladder Company No. 1, do hereby
mutually agree to disband, and thereby close
our services as volunteer firemen of the city
of Alameda on the 31st day of October, 1901,
at 12 o'clock midnight. Â¦
Whereas. We believe the city of. Alameda has
arrived at a period In wealth and Importance
when it is fully capable of providing for all
departments' of the - municipal -.service; and
whereas, Citizen Hook and Ladder Company
No. 1 has at all times kept up an active mem
bership and a full complement of members,
never less than fifteen (13). which number ! is
at present enrolled; and whereas. Citizen Hook
and Ladder Company No. 1 has given a quar
ter of a century voluntary service to this city
and its eitlzens, we believe that we should now
retire in favor of a paid department. There
fore
ALAMEDA. Aug. 6.â The City Trustees
were astounded last night .when .they re
ceived ; a communication from Citizen
Hook and Ladder Company No. 1 an
nouncing its Intention to withdraw' from
the volunteer flre' department at the end
of three months. As the company has
always , been the most active in the de
partment and the one organization that
kept the J volunteers together, the City
Trustees look upon this action as/the be
ginning' ' of the end of the volunteer aer
vice. The communication from the com
pany, which was in the form of resolu
tions passed at its last meeting, follows:
Beginning of the End of
Volunteer Service in
Alameda.
Hook and Ladder Com
pany Resigns Prom the
Fire^Departinent.
PAID SERVICE
MAY BE FORCED

At a meeting held last evening at the
residence of Mrs. Jennie Bright Holden,
522 Oak street, the San Francisco Associ
ation for the Oral Education of Deaf
Children was organized. The object of
the association is to promote the educa
tion and welfare of deaf children. Henry
Walker was elected president of the asso
ciation, Mrs. H. J. Cassady secretary
and Samuel Brleger treasurer.
To Educate the Deaf.
Considerable commotion was caused at
the ferry last night by John McGee. a
long whiskered old man, who scattered a
crowd of several hundred people with a
toy cap pistol and gave Officer Henne
berry a chaseÂ»for several blocks.
McGee is a laborer on the water front
and last night imbibed considerable fire
water" in the maritime resorts. After
leaving a saloon on East street, near Mis
sion, he was attracted by the crowds at
the ferry and soon leaned against one or
the pillars observing the people come and
go A number of boys commenced to poke
fun at him and, passing in a line, each
pulled his whiskers, which were long and
forcczv
Finally the old man became desperate
and pulling a glistening .weapon from his
pocket flourished it. The crowd of pas
sengers was just landing from an Oak
land boat and the pistol wielder darted
among them threatening to send every
body to the Morsue. Women screamed
and men rushed abort pellmelL A call
for the police was sent to the Harbor Sta
tion and Officer W. Henneberry respond
ed When the policeman came upon the
ooene McGee started to run and did not
stop until the minion of the law was upon
him at Market and Drumm streets. Mc-
Gee placed his weapon against the offi
cer's breast and snapped the trigger, but
he did no harm, for the gun that had
scared the crowd was only a toy cap pis
tol. " '
John McGee .Makes a
, Scene at the Ferry ; :
Building.

S3CTRET OF SAN JOSE FIGHT.
said John "W. McCarthy, secretary of the
Fire Commission, yesterday, "and neither
Kelly nor Rainey will have the services
of any employe of the Fire Department on
the day of the primaries or prior thereto.
"The commission has gone so far as to
deny vacations during the first half of
August, that none of the men may get a
period of freedom in which to do politics.
"When a sick leave is asked the board
requires the certificates of two physicians
before the applicant's request for a lay-off
on' account of disability is granted; then
the person securing the leave must remain
in his house during the whole period he
is off duty. The ofllcers of the department
are required to call upon all sick men to
see that they are so confined, and I visit
them myself and report to the board.
"From now until after the primaries
every fireman in the San Francisco de
partment who Is able to perform his duties
will be housed in and those who are sick
must stay at their homes. No politics
will be done by them.
"President Watt personally issued or
ders for the captains that their men
should be allowed just time enough away
from the houses on primary day, except
in case of fire, in which to cast their bal
lots; that any man staying longer without
permission would lose his place, and that
any captain who gave a man leave to stay
away longer on the day of the primaries
would be removed. These orders are abso
lute and any dlsobeyal will result in a loss
of position. Chief Sullivan and his imme
diate subordinates have been cautioned to
see that the orders are obeyed. There will
be no fireman doing politics for the bosses
on August 13."
It is evident that the Phelan machine Is
not going to allow the Fire Department
to be used by its old master, Rainey. The
Phelan combine can never itself win the
love of the men on the.fire company rolls,
but it has the power "at present to keep
them locked up when Rainey wants work
done and seems determined to do it.
Mr. Harrison was in favor of proceeding
with the election, but the members were
desirous of hearing from his lips how
the club has progressed during the past
year. He told of the obstacles that con
fronted the present, board when they en
tered office, of unpaid bills and an out
standing indebtedness of $29,000; delin-.
quent membership and lack of credit. He
then dwelt on the work of rehabilitation
and told how the board had brought the
club to its present high standing and of
the improvements effected during the past
year.
Colonel Beck then placed Messrs. Leigh
ton, Hale, Hewson, Melrose and Cunning
ham in nomination. Mr. Gleason seconded
the nomination. Clement Bennett moved
that nominations be closed and the regu
lar ticket was thus selected by unanimous
vote. . .
President Harrison called the meeting
to order and directed Secretary John El
liott to read the sectoina of the by-laws
under which the meeting was held. The
chairman* then appointed H. J. Crocker
and Mr. Thornton . tellers. Their services
were unnecessary, as there was but one
ticket in the field.
It is a foregone conclusion that the fol
lowing officers will be unanimously, elect

Article XVI, section 32. No member of the
Board of Police Commissioners and no member
of the Board of Fire Commissioners and no of
ficer, subordinate or employe of the Police De
partment or of the. Fire Department shall be
a member of any partisan convention, the pur
pose of which is to nominate candidates for
otflce; nor shall either of them, directly or
Indirectly, electioneer, by soliciting: votes or
otherwise, for or against any candidate for
office at any election, or for or against any
candidate for nomination before any political
convention, or for or against any candidate
for delegate to such convention at any pri
mary election; nor shall either of them be a
member of any committee, club or organiza
tion the purpose of which Is to nominate or
indorse candidates for office i at any i election;
nor in any way attempt to Influence or control
such committee, club or organization while
nominating or- indorsing said candidates;- nor
take any Dart in the control, management or
distribution of the political patronage of any
public officer; nor shall any. member of either
of said boards, or any officer, Â¦. subordinate or
employe of- either of said departments, directly
or indirectly, attempt to 'control or in any
manner - influence the action of any officer,
subordinate or employe of . either of said de
partments at any "general, special or primary
election; and no officer, subordinate or em
ploye of either of said departments shall levy,'
collect or pay any amount of money as an as
sessment or contribution for- political pur
poses. Any person violating any of the pro
visions of this section shall 'be removed forth
with from his office or employment. If the
violation be by a - member of either of said
boards the Mayor must remove such member;
and If by an officer, employe or subordinate of
either of said departments, . then the board
whose officer, employe or subordinate has been
guilty of such violation must remove such offi
cer, employe or' subordinate; and if such
board fail or refuse to make such removal,
then the . Mayor must remove all . members
of the board who have so fallod or refused."
Acting upon our understanding of thU sec
tion of the charter, the members of this board
have refrained from joining any political club
or similar, organization, and in. view of the
strong language of the section referred to, -it Is
our purpose to absolutely refrain from exer
cising, or attempting to exercise any influence
for or against any candidate for office or dele
gate to convention, or otherwise,* reserving
only that prerogative of Â¦ every American citi
zenâ the right to vote. ' ;
. The commission will insist Â¦ upon - absolute
compliance with the above quoted < section of
the charter by every officer,' member and-em
ploye of this department, and in dealing with
any one charged .with . a violation thereof will
enforce the law in letter and spirit without
fear or favor. ; '.''... ; ', !
We quite understand howdlfflcult It 'will" be
for those who have heretofore taken an active
interest in political matters to passively watch
the progress of events 'without' participation. : ,
. Having called your attention to the' matter,
we feel certain ' thaj you will . be governed by
the clear intent of the; charter â which' Is the
will of : the people. We would â¢ also refer you
to rule 4 of the general 'rules (page 40) of the
department covering the same matter, viola
tions of which" will be punished .with the. full
penalty.. .*.. Â¦Â¦> / â¢ . -. . - - . . : ,
The officers and members of the denartment
arÂ© ; hereby instructed to. report .. to thÂ© com
mission any infringement of this rule.' By order

discussed and the matter was left, in the

gate personally work to secure two
friends to be on duty at the polling booths
In the precinct.

of delegates, and Melville. Herman asked

their services on election. day and prevent
the slightest chance of fraud at the polls.

Petitions in Insolvency.
Urs. Alice Flood appeared before Judge
Cabaniss yesterday and asked him ro
withdraw the warrant she had sworn
to on Monday for the arrest of
Fred Lutton on the charge of grand
larceny. She said she had found
the diamond ring and money which she
accused Lutton of having stolen under the
sofa in her room. The Judge declined to
withdraw the warrant, and Detective T.
B. Gibson, who was on the case, intimated
that the woman may be prosecuted for
perjury in swearing to the warrant.
Mrs. Flood May Be Prosecuted.

11 mary League tickets a-t the polls
I next Tuesday will insure a pro
il gressive and . honest administra
tion of municipal affairs under
the auspices of the Republican party. All
the signs of the hour point to utter defeat
of the allied bosses. It Is noted with

pendent clubs have welcomed fusion with
the district organizations of the league.
Last night all the anti-boss elements of
the Thirty-sixth ana Thirty-eighth dis
tricts combined. This fusion will accom
plish the defeat of the bosses in these
two important districts. Last evening
when Max Goldberg heard of the union
of all the anti-bors elements in the
Tiiirty-eighth he sallied forth to find
John C. Lynch. Before midnight all the
bosses knew that their last hope of divid
ing the anti-boss forces in the district
had vanished. , ,
Consternation was created in the ,boss
camp when the news spread that all the
elements of good citizenship in the Thir
ty-sixth District were consolidating to
down the boss ticwet next Tuesday.
Last night ten of the Republican Pri
mary League district tickets were filed in
the Registrar's office. The remaining tick
ets will be filed to-night. Boss Kelly hied
six of his tickets yesterday. He is hav
ing trouble to find respectable men who
are willing to lend their names to -his
ticket. Surprise was expressed last night
that G. W. Kingsbury, proprietor of the
Lick House had permitted his name to
head the Kelly-Crimmins ticket in the
Forty-fifth District.
Leading citizens who do not want their
names associated with those of corrupt
bosses should take the earliest opportu
nity to scan the boss ticket in their re
spective districts. Time remains for
good citizens to withdraw from all sem
blance of sympathy or fellowship with
corrupt political rounders.

Mr. Bergez, when seen, said that pov
erty should not have led Grogan to such
a desperate measure, as he had hundreds
of friends in this city who would have
been only too glad to assist him if his
pride would have permitted him to have
made his wants known. â¢
Grogan, whenever he said anything at
all about his past, always claimed that he
was related to a noble family of Europe.
Just before taking his life he write to
John Bergez of this city, telling of the
deed he had under contemplation and giv
ing ill health and poverty as the causes
that resulted in the tragedy. Inclosed in
the letter was his card, which reads:
Alejandro E. Grogan, Baron de Norden
flycht.
Finally Grogan's funds began to run
low, and some time ago he bade good
by to his friends, telling them he was
going to Chile, where he expected to re
coup his fortunes. This was the last heard
of him until yesterday's dispatch printed
in The Call told them of his sad end in
the little village where he had lived for
gotten within a couple of hours' ride of
the city where he had once reigned a
prince.
It was a little, very little, item in yes
terday's Call. So short was' this tele
graphic bit of news that told of the self
inflicted death of an elderly man at Los
Gatos, that It may have escaped the no
tice of those who hastily skim over the
pages of their paper between the break
fast hour and the daily trip to the office.
Yet to those of the old-timers who did see
the article in question it evoked a host
of reminiscences which carried them back
to the palmy days of bonanza excitement,
when every mans pocket was lined with
the rich product or California's golden
lulls, iina wnen San .Francisco was the
second city or importance in the world 01
Bohemiaâ the other one being Paris.
'ihe man who Killed himself by taking
a dose or prussic acid was Alexander hi.
Â«jrogan, or *\L,ittle iirogan," as he was
known to the multitude of friends that
were His when everything was coming his
way f when he was the miniature Beau
Brummel of the world that lives by gas
light and ttie arbiter elegantiarum among
me boys who set up late.
Grogan, during his latter days in San
Francisco, was a man of over 50 years
of age. His carefully dyed hair, pertectly
ntting clotr.ee, trim figure and generally
well groomefl appearance, however, so
well assisted time in the effort to preserve
him that he hardly looked to be more than
iO. To most of his friends and acquaint
ances he was a complete mystery, and
even his older and more intimate com
panions can now recall but little concern
ing him.
St'hough his name was Grogan, of un
doubted Celtic origin, yet the man himself
was certainly a continental " European,
though from what part of Euro'pe he came
was impossible to tell, as he spoke half
a dozen languages with equal fluency. The
name of Grogan came to him from Dr.
Alexander Grogan, a wealthy physician of
early days, by whom he was adopted. The
physician died leaving a fortune verging
close to a million, but this adopted son,
owing to an estrangement that took place
between the two men shortly before the
doctor's death* was cut off with about
a hundred thousand dollars, and on this
sum he started out to continue his career
as a good fellow. His wide knowledge
of the world, his thorough acquaintance
with all the accomplishments of - a bon
vivant, his wit, generosity and general
good nature had already made him a
warm favorite, and for a long time he
was one of the most sought after men in
the city.
Sad and Mysterious Story of
a Life That Ended in .
Despair.
Great Favorite in the
Bohemian Days of
San Francisco.
THE PASSING OF
"LITTLE GROGAN"
